11	Internet X.509 Public Key Infrastructure - Algorithm Identifiers for the
12	       Module-Lattice-Based Digital Signature Algorithm (ML-DSA)
13	               draft-ietf-lamps-dilithium-certificates-11

15	Abstract

17	   Digital signatures are used within X.509 certificates, Certificate
18	   Revocation Lists (CRLs), and to sign messages.  This document
19	   describes the conventions for using FIPS 204, the Module-Lattice-
20	   Based Digital Signature Algorithm (ML-DSA) in Internet X.509
21	   certificates and certificate revocation lists.  The conventions for
22	   the associated signatures, subject public keys, and private key are
23	   also described.

27	   This note is to be removed before publishing as an RFC.

106	1.  Introduction

108	   The Module-Lattice-Based Digital Signature Algorithm (ML-DSA) is a
109	   quantum-resistant digital signature scheme standardized by the US
110	   National Institute of Standards and Technology (NIST) PQC project
111	   [NIST-PQC] in [FIPS204].  This document specifies the use of the ML-
112	   DSA in Public Key Infrastructure X.509 (PKIX) certificates and
113	   Certificate Revocation Lists (CRLs) at three security levels: ML-DSA-
114	   44, ML-DSA-65, and ML-DSA-87.

116	   [FIPS204] defines two variants of ML-DSA: a pure and a prehash
117	   variant.  Only the former is specified in this document.  See
118	   Section 10.1 for the rationale.  The pure variant of ML-DSA supports
119	   the typical prehash flow.  Refer to Appendix D for more details.

121	   Prior to standardisation, ML-DSA was known as Dilithium.  ML-DSA and
122	   Dilithium are not compatible.

124	1.1.  Requirements Language

126	   The key words "MUST", "MUST NOT", "REQUIRED", "SHALL", "SHALL NOT",
127	   "SHOULD", "SHOULD NOT", "RECOMMENDED", "NOT RECOMMENDED", "MAY", and
128	   "OPTIONAL" in this document are to be interpreted as described in
129	   BCP 14 [RFC2119] [RFC8174] when, and only when, they appear in all
130	   capitals, as shown here.

132	2.  Identifiers

134	   The AlgorithmIdentifier type is defined in [RFC5912] as follows:

136	    AlgorithmIdentifier{ALGORITHM-TYPE, ALGORITHM-TYPE:AlgorithmSet} ::=
137	      SEQUENCE {
138	        algorithm   ALGORITHM-TYPE.id({AlgorithmSet}),
139	        parameters  ALGORITHM-TYPE.
140	                      Params({AlgorithmSet}{@algorithm}) OPTIONAL
141	     }
142	      |  NOTE: The above syntax is from [RFC5912] and is compatible with
143	      |  the 2021 ASN.1 syntax [X680].  See [RFC5280] for the 1988 ASN.1
144	      |  syntax.

146	   The fields in AlgorithmIdentifier have the following meanings:

148	   *  algorithm identifies the cryptographic algorithm with an object
149	      identifier (OID).

151	   *  parameters, which are optional, are the associated parameters for
152	      the algorithm identifier in the algorithm field.

154	   The OIDs are:

156	      id-ml-dsa-44 OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { joint-iso-itu-t(2)
157	               country(16) us(840) organization(1) gov(101) csor(3)
158	               nistAlgorithm(4) sigAlgs(3) id-ml-dsa-44(17) }

160	      id-ml-dsa-65 OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { joint-iso-itu-t(2)
161	               country(16) us(840) organization(1) gov(101) csor(3)
162	               nistAlgorithm(4) sigAlgs(3) id-ml-dsa-65(18) }

164	      id-ml-dsa-87 OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { joint-iso-itu-t(2)
165	               country(16) us(840) organization(1) gov(101) csor(3)
166	               nistAlgorithm(4) sigAlgs(3) id-ml-dsa-87(19) }

168	   The contents of the parameters component for each algorithm MUST be
169	   absent.

171	3.  ML-DSA Signatures in PKIX

173	   ML-DSA is a digital signature scheme built upon the Fiat-Shamir-with-
174	   aborts framework [Fiat-Shamir].  The security is based upon the
175	   hardness of lattice problems over module lattices [Dilithium].  ML-
176	   DSA provides three parameter sets for the NIST PQC security
177	   categories 2, 3 and 5.

179	   Signatures are used in a number of different ASN.1 structures.  As
180	   shown in the ASN.1 representation from [RFC5280] below, in an X.509
181	   certificate, a signature is encoded with an algorithm identifier in
182	   the signatureAlgorithm attribute and a signatureValue attribute that
183	   contains the actual signature.

185	     Certificate  ::=  SIGNED{ TBSCertificate }

187	     SIGNED{ToBeSigned} ::= SEQUENCE {
188	        toBeSigned           ToBeSigned,
189	        algorithmIdentifier  SEQUENCE {
190	            algorithm        SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM.
191	                               &id({SignatureAlgorithms}),
192	            parameters       SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM.
193	                               &Params({SignatureAlgorithms}
194	                                 {@algorithmIdentifier.algorithm})
195	                                   OPTIONAL
196	        },
197	        signature BIT STRING (CONTAINING SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM.&Value(
198	                                 {SignatureAlgorithms}
199	                                 {@algorithmIdentifier.algorithm}))
200	     }

202	   Signatures are also used in the CRL list ASN.1 representation from
203	   [RFC5280] below.  In a X.509 CRL, a signature is encoded with an
204	   algorithm identifier in the signatureAlgorithm attribute and a
205	   signatureValue attribute that contains the actual signature.

207	      CertificateList  ::=  SIGNED{ TBSCertList }

209	   The following SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ASN.1 classes are for ML-DSA-44,
210	   ML-DSA-65, and ML-DSA-87:

212	     sa-ml-dsa-44 SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
213	       IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-44
214	       PARAMS ARE absent
215	       PUBLIC-KEYS { pk-ml-dsa-44 }
216	       SMIME-CAPS { IDENTIFIED BY id-ml-dsa-44 }
217	       }

219	     sa-ml-dsa-65 SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
220	       IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-65
221	       PARAMS ARE absent
222	       PUBLIC-KEYS { pk-ml-dsa-65 }
223	       SMIME-CAPS { IDENTIFIED BY id-ml-dsa-65 }
224	       }

226	     sa-ml-dsa-87 SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
227	       IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-87
228	       PARAMS ARE absent
229	       PUBLIC-KEYS { pk-ml-dsa-87 }
230	       SMIME-CAPS { IDENTIFIED BY id-ml-dsa-87 }
231	       }

233	      |  NOTE: The above syntax is from [RFC5912] and is compatible with
234	      |  the 2021 ASN.1 syntax [X680].  See [RFC5280] for the 1988 ASN.1
235	      |  syntax.

237	   The identifiers defined in Section 2 can be used as the
238	   AlgorithmIdentifier in the signatureAlgorithm field in the sequence
239	   Certificate/CertificateList and the signature field in the sequence
240	   TBSCertificate/TBSCertList in certificates and CRLs, respectively,
241	   [RFC5280].  The parameters of these signature algorithms MUST be
242	   absent, as explained in Section 2.  That is, the AlgorithmIdentifier
243	   SHALL be a SEQUENCE of one component, the OID id-ml-dsa-*.

245	   The signatureValue field contains the corresponding ML-DSA signature
246	   computed upon the ASN.1 DER encoded tbsCertificate/tbsCertList
247	   [RFC5280].  The optional context string (ctx) parameter as defined in
248	   Section 5.2 of [FIPS204] is left to its default value: the empty
249	   string.

251	   Conforming Certification Authority (CA) implementations MUST specify
252	   the algorithms explicitly by using the OIDs specified in Section 2
253	   when encoding ML-DSA signatures in certificates and CRLs.  Conforming
254	   client implementations that process certificates and CRLs using ML-
255	   DSA MUST recognize the corresponding OIDs.  Encoding rules for ML-DSA
256	   signature values are specified in Section 2.

258	4.  ML-DSA Public Keys in PKIX

260	   In the X.509 certificate, the subjectPublicKeyInfo field has the
261	   SubjectPublicKeyInfo type, which has the following ASN.1 syntax:

263	     SubjectPublicKeyInfo {PUBLIC-KEY: IOSet} ::= SEQUENCE {
264	         algorithm        AlgorithmIdentifier {PUBLIC-KEY, {IOSet}},
265	         subjectPublicKey BIT STRING
266	     }

268	      |  NOTE: The above syntax is from [RFC5912] and is compatible with
269	      |  the 2021 ASN.1 syntax [X680].  See [RFC5280] for the 1988 ASN.1
270	      |  syntax.

272	   The fields in SubjectPublicKeyInfo have the following meaning:

274	   *  algorithm is the algorithm identifier and parameters for the
275	      public key (see above).

277	   *  subjectPublicKey contains the public key.

279	   The PUBLIC-KEY ASN.1 types for ML-DSA are defined here:

281	     pk-ml-dsa-44 PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
282	       IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-44
283	       -- KEY no ASN.1 wrapping --
284	       CERT-KEY-USAGE
285	         { digitalSignature, nonRepudiation, keyCertSign, cRLSign }
286	       PRIVATE-KEY ML-DSA-44-PrivateKey }  -- defined in Section 6

288	     pk-ml-dsa-65 PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
289	       IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-65
290	       -- KEY no ASN.1 wrapping --
291	       CERT-KEY-USAGE
292	         { digitalSignature, nonRepudiation, keyCertSign, cRLSign }
293	       PRIVATE-KEY ML-DSA-65-PrivateKey }  -- defined in Section 6

295	     pk-ml-dsa-87 PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
296	       IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-87
297	       -- KEY no ASN.1 wrapping --
298	       CERT-KEY-USAGE
299	         { digitalSignature, nonRepudiation, keyCertSign, cRLSign }
300	       PRIVATE-KEY ML-DSA-87-PrivateKey }  -- defined in Section 6

302	     ML-DSA-44-PublicKey ::= OCTET STRING (SIZE (1312))

304	     ML-DSA-65-PublicKey ::= OCTET STRING (SIZE (1952))

306	     ML-DSA-87-PublicKey ::= OCTET STRING (SIZE (2592))

308	      |  NOTE: The above syntax is from [RFC5912] and is compatible with
309	      |  the 2021 ASN.1 syntax [X680].  See [RFC5280] for the 1988 ASN.1
310	      |  syntax.

312	   Algorithm 22 in Section 7.2 of [FIPS204] defines the raw byte string
313	   encoding of an ML-DSA public key.  When used in a
314	   SubjectPublicKeyInfo type, the subjectPublicKey BIT STRING contains
315	   this raw byte string encoding of the public key.

317	   When an ML-DSA public key appears outside of a SubjectPublicKeyInfo
318	   type in an environment that uses ASN.1 encoding, it can be encoded as
319	   an OCTET STRING by using the ML-DSA-44-PublicKey, ML-DSA-
320	   65-PublicKey, and ML-DSA-87-PublicKey types corresponding to the
321	   correct key size.

323	   [RFC5958] describes the Asymmetric Key Package's OneAsymmetricKey
324	   type for encoding asymmetric keypairs.  When an ML-DSA private key or
325	   keypair is encoded as a OneAsymmetricKey, it follows the description
326	   in Section 6.

328	   When the ML-DSA private key appears outside of an Asymmetric Key
329	   Package in an environment that uses ASN.1 encoding, it can be encoded
330	   using one of the the ML-DSA-PrivateKey CHOICE formats defined in
331	   Section 6.  The seed format is RECOMMENDED as it efficiently stores
332	   both the private and public key.

334	   Appendix C contains example ML-DSA public keys encoded using the
335	   textual encoding defined in [RFC7468].

337	5.  Key Usage Bits

339	   The intended application for the key is indicated in the keyUsage
340	   certificate extension; see Section 4.2.1.3 of [RFC5280].  If the
341	   keyUsage extension is present in a certificate that indicates id-ml-
342	   dsa-* in the SubjectPublicKeyInfo, then at least one of following
343	   MUST be present:

345	     digitalSignature; or
346	     nonRepudiation; or
347	     keyCertSign; or
348	     cRLSign.

350	   If the keyUsage extension is present in a certificate that indicates
351	   id-ml-dsa-* in the SubjectPublicKeyInfo, then the following MUST NOT
352	   be present:

354	      keyEncipherment; or
355	      dataEncipherment; or
356	      keyAgreement; or
357	      encipherOnly; or
358	      decipherOnly.

360	   Requirements about the keyUsage extension bits defined in [RFC5280]
361	   still apply.

363	6.  Private Key Format

365	   [FIPS204] specifies two formats for an ML-DSA private key: a 32-octet
366	   seed (xi) and an (expanded) private key.  The expanded private key
367	   (and public key) is computed from the seed using ML-
368	   DSA.KeyGen_internal(xi) (algorithm 6).

370	   "Asymmetric Key Packages" [RFC5958] describes how to encode a private
371	   key in a structure that both identifies what algorithm the private
372	   key is for and allows for the public key and additional attributes
373	   about the key to be included as well.  For illustration, the ASN.1
374	   structure OneAsymmetricKey is replicated below.

376	     OneAsymmetricKey ::= SEQUENCE {
377	       version                  Version,
378	       privateKeyAlgorithm      SEQUENCE {
379	       algorithm                PUBLIC-KEY.&id({PublicKeySet}),
380	       parameters               PUBLIC-KEY.&Params({PublicKeySet}
381	                                  {@privateKeyAlgorithm.algorithm})
382	                                     OPTIONAL}
383	       privateKey               OCTET STRING (CONTAINING
384	                                  PUBLIC-KEY.&PrivateKey({PublicKeySet}
385	                                    {@privateKeyAlgorithm.algorithm})),
386	       attributes           [0] Attributes OPTIONAL,
387	       ...,
388	       [[2: publicKey       [1] BIT STRING (CONTAINING
389	                                  PUBLIC-KEY.&Params({PublicKeySet}
390	                                    {@privateKeyAlgorithm.algorithm})
391	                                    OPTIONAL,
392	       ...
393	     }

395	      |  NOTE: The above syntax is from [RFC5958] and is compatible with
396	      |  the 2021 ASN.1 syntax [X680].

398	   For ML-DSA private keys, the privateKey field in OneAsymmetricKey
399	   contains one of the following DER-encoded CHOICE structures.  The
400	   seed format is a fixed 32 byte OCTET STRING (34 bytes total with the
401	   0x8020 tag and length) for all security levels, while the expandedKey
402	   and both formats vary in size by security level:

404	   ML-DSA-44-PrivateKey ::= CHOICE {
405	     seed [0] OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
406	     expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (2560)),
407	     both SEQUENCE {
408	         seed OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
409	         expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (2560))
410	         }
411	     }

413	   ML-DSA-65-PrivateKey ::= CHOICE {
414	     seed [0] OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
415	     expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4032)),
416	     both SEQUENCE {
417	         seed OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
418	         expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4032))
419	         }
420	     }

422	   ML-DSA-87-PrivateKey ::= CHOICE {
423	     seed [0] OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
424	     expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4896)),
425	     both SEQUENCE {
426	         seed OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
427	         expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4896))
428	         }
429	     }

431	      |  NOTE: The above syntax is from [RFC5912] and is compatible with
432	      |  the 2021 ASN.1 syntax [X680].  See [RFC5280] for the 1988 ASN.1
433	      |  syntax.

435	   The CHOICE allows three representations of the private key:

437	   1.  The seed format (tag [0]) contains just the 32-byte seed value
438	       (xi) from which both the expanded private key and public key can
439	       be derived using ML-DSA.KeyGen_internal(xi).

441	   2.  The expandedKey format contains the expanded private key that was
442	       derived from the seed.

444	   3.  The both format contains both the seed and expanded private key,
445	       allowing for for interoperability; some may want to use and
446	       retain the seed and others may only support expanded private
447	       keys.

449	   When encoding an ML-DSA private key in a OneAsymmetricKey object, any
450	   of these three formats may be used, though the seed format is
451	   RECOMMENDED for storage efficiency.

453	   The privateKeyAlgorithm field uses the AlgorithmIdentifier structure
454	   with the appropriate OID as defined in Section 2.  If present, the
455	   publicKey field will hold the encoded public key as defined in
456	   Section 4.

458	   NOTE: While the private key can be stored in multiple formats, the
459	   seed-only format is RECOMMENDED as it is the most compact
460	   representation.  Both the expanded private key and the public key can
461	   be deterministically derived from the seed using ML-
462	   DSA.KeyGen_internal(xi).  Alternatively, the public key can be
463	   generated from the private key.  While the publicKey field and
464	   expandedKey format are technically redundant when using the seed-only
465	   format, they MAY be included to enable keypair consistency checks
466	   during import operations.

468	   When parsing the private key, the ASN.1 tag explicitly indicates
469	   which variant of CHOICE is present.  Implementations should use the
470	   context-specific tag IMPLICIT [0] (raw value 0x80) for seed, OCTET
471	   STRING (0x04) for expandedKey, and SEQUENCE (0x30) for both to parse
472	   the private key, rather than any other heuristic like length of the
473	   enclosing OCTET STRING.

475	   Appendix C contains example ML-DSA private keys encoded using the
476	   textual encoding defined in [RFC7468].

478	7.  IANA Considerations

480	   For the ASN.1 module in Appendix A, IANA is requested to assign an
481	   object identifier (OID) for the module identifier (TBD1) with a
482	   Description of "id-mod-x509-ml-dsa-2025".  The OID for the module
483	   should be allocated in the "SMI Security for PKIX Module Identifier"
484	   registry (1.3.6.1.5.5.7.0).

486	8.  Implementation Considerations

488	   An ML-DSA.KeyGen seed (xi) represents the RECOMMENDED format for
489	   storing and transmitting ML-DSA private keys.  This format is
490	   explicitly permitted by [FIPS204] as an acceptable representation of
491	   a keypair.  In particular, generating the seed in one cryptographic
492	   module and then importing or exporting it into another cryptographic
493	   module is allowed.  The internal key generation function ML-
494	   DSA.KeyGen_internal(xi) can be accessed for this purpose.

496	   Note also that unlike other private key compression methods in other
497	   algorithms, expanding a private key from a seed is a one-way
498	   function, meaning that once a full key is expanded from seed and the
499	   seed discarded, the seed cannot be re-created even if the full
500	   expanded private key is available.  For this reason it is RECOMMENDED
501	   that implementations retain and export the seed, even when also
502	   exporting the expanded private key.  ML-DSA seed extraction can be
503	   implemented by including the seed xi randomly generated at line 1 of
504	   Algorithm 1 ML-DSA.KeyGen in the returned output.

506	9.  Private Key Consistency Testing

508	   When receiving a private key that contains both the seed and the
509	   expandedKey, the recipient SHOULD perform a seed consistency check to
510	   ensure that the sender properly generated the private key.
511	   Recipients that do not perform this seed consistency check avoid
512	   keygen and compare operations, but are unable to ensure that the seed
513	   and expandedKey match.

515	   If the check is done and the seed and the expandedKey are not
516	   consistent, the recipient MUST reject the private key as malformed.

518	   The seed consistency check consists of regenerating the expanded form
519	   from the seed via ML-DSA.KeyGen_internal and ensuring it is bytewise
520	   equal to the value presented in the private key.

522	   Appendix C.4 includes some examples of inconsistent seeds and
523	   expanded private keys.

525	10.  Security Considerations

527	   The Security Considerations section of [RFC5280] applies to this
528	   specification as well.

530	   The ML-DSA signature scheme is strongly unforgeable under chosen
531	   message attacks (SUF-CMA).  For the purpose of estimating security
532	   strength, it has been assumed that the attacker has access to
533	   signatures for no more than 2^{64} chosen messages.

535	   ML-DSA depends on high quality random numbers that are suitable for
536	   use in cryptography.  The use of inadequate pseudo-random number
537	   generators (PRNGs) to generate such values can significantly
538	   undermine various security properties.  For instance, using an
539	   inadequate PRNG for key generation, might allow an attacker to
540	   efficiently recover the private key by trying a small set of
541	   possibilities, rather than brute force search the whole keyspace.
542	   The generation of random numbers of a sufficient level of quality for
543	   use in cryptography is difficult, and [RFC4086] offers important
544	   guidance in this area.

546	   In the design of ML-DSA, care has been taken to make side-channel
547	   resilience easier to achieve.  For instance, ML-DSA does not depend
548	   on Gaussian sampling.  Implementations must still take great care not
549	   to leak information via various side channels.  While deliberate
550	   design decisions such as these can help to deliver a greater ease of
551	   secure implementation - particularly against side-channel attacks -
552	   it does not necessarily provide resistance to more powerful attacks
553	   such as differential power analysis.  Some amount of side-channel
554	   leakage has been demonstrated in parts of the signing algorithm
555	   (specifically the bit-unpacking function), from which a demonstration
556	   of key recovery has been made over a large sample of signatures.
557	   Masking countermeasures exist for ML-DSA, but come with a performance
558	   overhead.

560	   ML-DSA offers both deterministic and randomized signing.  Signatures
561	   generated with either mode are compatible and a verifyer can't tell
562	   them apart.  In the deterministic case, a signature only depends on
563	   the private key and the message to be signed.  This makes the
564	   implementation easier to test and does not require a randomness
565	   source during signing.  In the randomized case, signing mixes in a
566	   256-bit random string from an approved random bit generator (RBG).
567	   When randomized, ML-DSA is easier to harden against fault and
568	   hardware side-channel attacks.

570	   A security property also associated with digital signatures is non-
571	   repudiation.  Non-repudiation refers to the assurance that the owner
572	   of a signature key pair that was capable of generating an existing
573	   signature corresponding to certain data cannot convincingly deny
574	   having signed the data, unless its private key was compromised.  The
575	   digital signature scheme ML-DSA possess three security properties
576	   beyond unforgeability, that are associated with non-repudiation.
577	   These are exclusive ownership, message-bound signatures, and non-
578	   resignability.  These properties are based tightly on the assumed
579	   collision resistance of the hash function used (in this case SHAKE-
580	   256).  A full discussion of these properties in ML-DSA can be found
581	   at [CDFFJ21].

583	10.1.  Rationale for disallowing HashML-DSA

585	   The HashML-DSA mode defined in Section 5.4 of [FIPS204] MUST NOT be
586	   used; in other words, public keys identified by id-hash-ml-dsa-44-
587	   with-sha512, id-hash-ml-dsa-65-with-sha512, and id-hash-ml-dsa-87-
588	   with-sha512 MUST NOT be in X.509 certificates used for CRLs, OCSP,
589	   certificate issuance and related PKIX protocols.  This restriction is
590	   primarily to increase interoperability.

592	   ML-DSA and HashML-DSA are incompatible algorithms that require
593	   different Verify() routines.  This introduces the complexity of
594	   informing the verifier whether to use ML-DSA.Verify() or HashML-
595	   DSA.Verify().  Additionally, since the same OIDs are used to identify
596	   the ML-DSA public keys and ML-DSA signature algorithms, an
597	   implementation would need to commit a given public key to be either
598	   of type ML-DSA or HashML-DSA at the time of certificate creation.
599	   This is anticipated to cause operational issues in contexts where the
600	   operator does not know whether the key will need to produce pure or
601	   pre-hashed signatures at key generation time.  The External Mu mode
602	   described in Appendix D avoids all of these operational concerns.

604	   A minor security reason for disallowing HashML-DSA is that the design
605	   of the ML-DSA algorithm provides enhanced resistance against
606	   collision attacks, compared with HashML-DSA or conventional RSA or
607	   ECDSA signature algorithms.  Specifically, ML-DSA prepends the
608	   SHAKE256 hash of the public key (tr) to the message to-be-signed
609	   prior to hashing, as described in line 6 of Algorithm 7 of [FIPS204].
610	   This means that in the unlikely discovery of a collision attack
611	   against the SHA-3 family, an attacker would have to perform a public-
612	   key-specific collision search in order to find message pairs such
613	   that H(tr || m1) = H(tr || m2) since a direct hash collision H(m1) =
614	   H(m2) will not suffice.  HashML-DSA removes this enhanced security
615	   property.  In spite of its lack of targeted collision protection, the
616	   practical security risk of using HashML-DSA in X.509 signatures would
617	   be immaterial.  That is because a hash of the issuing CA's public key
618	   is already included in the Authority Key Identifier (AKI) extension
619	   which is signed as part of the tbsCertificate structure.  Even when
620	   it is a SHA-1 hash, general second pre-images against the AKI hash of
621	   existing issuing CAs would be impractical.

718	Appendix A.  ASN.1 Module

720	   This appendix includes the ASN.1 module [X680] for the ML-DSA.  Note
721	   that as per [RFC5280], certificates use the Distinguished Encoding
722	   Rules; see [X690].  This module imports objects from [RFC5912].

724	   <CODE BEGINS>
725	   X509-ML-DSA-2025
726	   { iso(1) identified-organization(3) dod(6)
727	     internet(1) security(5) mechanisms(5) pkix(7) id-mod(0)
728	     id-mod-x509-ml-dsa-2025(TBD1) }

730	   DEFINITIONS IMPLICIT TAGS ::= BEGIN

732	   EXPORTS ALL;

734	   IMPORTS

736	    PUBLIC-KEY, SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM
737	      FROM AlgorithmInformation-2009  -- [RFC 5912]
738	        { iso(1) identified-organization(3) dod(6) internet(1)
739	          security(5) mechanisms(5) pkix(7) id-mod(0)
740	          id-mod-algorithmInformation-02(58) } ;

742	   --
743	   -- ML-DSA Identifiers
744	   --

746	   nistAlgorithms OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { joint-iso-itu-t(2)
747	     country(16) us(840) organization(1) gov(101) csor(3)
748	     nistAlgorithms(4) }

750	   sigAlgs OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { nistAlgorithms 3 }

752	   id-ml-dsa-44 OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { sigAlgs 17 }

754	   id-ml-dsa-65 OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { sigAlgs 18 }

756	   id-ml-dsa-87 OBJECT IDENTIFIER ::= { sigAlgs 19 }

758	   --
759	   -- Public Key Algorithms
760	   --

762	   PublicKeys PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
763	     -- This expands PublicKeys from [RFC 5912]
764	     pk-ml-dsa-44 |
765	     pk-ml-dsa-65 |
766	     pk-ml-dsa-87,
767	     ...
768	   }

770	   --
771	   -- ML-DSA Public Keys
772	   --

774	   pk-ml-dsa-44 PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
775	     IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-44
776	     -- KEY no ASN.1 wrapping; 1312 octets --
777	     PARAMS ARE absent
778	     CERT-KEY-USAGE { digitalSignature, nonRepudiation,
779	                      keyCertSign, cRLSign }
780	     PRIVATE-KEY ML-DSA-44-PrivateKey
781	     }

783	   ML-DSA-44-PrivateKey ::= CHOICE {
784	     seed [0] OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
785	     expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (2560)),
786	     both SEQUENCE {
787	         seed OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
788	         expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (2560))
789	         }
790	     }

792	   pk-ml-dsa-65 PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
793	     IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-65
794	     -- KEY no ASN.1 wrapping; 1952 octets --
795	     PARAMS ARE absent
796	     CERT-KEY-USAGE { digitalSignature, nonRepudiation,
797	                      keyCertSign, cRLSign }
798	     PRIVATE-KEY ML-DSA-65-PrivateKey
799	     }

801	   ML-DSA-65-PrivateKey ::= CHOICE {
802	     seed [0] OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
803	     expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4032)),
804	     both SEQUENCE {
805	         seed OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
806	         expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4032))
807	         }
808	     }

810	   pk-ml-dsa-87 PUBLIC-KEY ::= {
811	     IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-87
812	     -- KEY no ASN.1 wrapping; 2592 octets --
813	     PARAMS ARE absent
814	     CERT-KEY-USAGE { digitalSignature, nonRepudiation,
815	                       keyCertSign, cRLSign }
816	     PRIVATE-KEY ML-DSA-87-PrivateKey
817	     }

819	   ML-DSA-87-PrivateKey ::= CHOICE {
820	     seed [0] OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
821	     expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4896)),
822	     both SEQUENCE {
823	         seed OCTET STRING (SIZE (32)),
824	         expandedKey OCTET STRING (SIZE (4896))
825	         }
826	     }

828	   ML-DSA-44-PublicKey ::= OCTET STRING (SIZE (1312))

830	   ML-DSA-65-PublicKey ::= OCTET STRING (SIZE (1952))

832	   ML-DSA-87-PublicKey ::= OCTET STRING (SIZE (2602))

834	   --
835	   -- Signature Algorithms
836	   --

838	   SignatureAlgorithms SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
839	     -- This expands SignatureAlgorithms from [RFC 5912]
840	     sa-ml-dsa-44 |
841	     sa-ml-dsa-65 |
842	     sa-ml-dsa-87,
843	     ... }

845	   --
846	   -- ML-DSA Signature Algorithm Identifiers
847	   --

849	   sa-ml-dsa-44 SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
850	     IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-44
851	     PARAMS ARE absent
852	     PUBLIC-KEYS { pk-ml-dsa-44 }
853	     SMIME-CAPS { IDENTIFIED BY id-ml-dsa-44 }
854	     }

856	   sa-ml-dsa-65 SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
857	     IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-65
858	     PARAMS ARE absent
859	     PUBLIC-KEYS { pk-ml-dsa-65 }
860	     SMIME-CAPS { IDENTIFIED BY id-ml-dsa-65 }
861	     }

863	   sa-ml-dsa-87 SIGNATURE-ALGORITHM ::= {
864	     IDENTIFIER id-ml-dsa-87
865	     PARAMS ARE absent
866	     PUBLIC-KEYS { pk-ml-dsa-87 }
867	     SMIME-CAPS { IDENTIFIED BY id-ml-dsa-87 }
868	     }

870	   END
871	   <CODE ENDS>

873	Appendix B.  Security Strengths

875	   Instead of defining the strength of a quantum algorithm in a
876	   traditional manner using the imprecise notion of bits of security,
877	   NIST has instead elected to define security levels by picking a
878	   reference scheme, which NIST expects to offer notable levels of
879	   resistance to both quantum and classical attack.  To wit, an
880	   algorithm that achieves NIST PQC security level 1 must require
881	   computational resources to break the relevant security property,
882	   which are greater than those required for a brute-force key search on
883	   AES-128.  Levels 3 and 5 use AES-192 and AES-256 as reference
884	   respectively.  Levels 2 and 4 use collision search for SHA-256 and
885	   SHA-384 as reference.

887	   The parameter sets defined for NIST security levels 2, 3 and 5 are
888	   listed in the Figure 1, along with the resulting signature size,
889	   public key, and private key sizes in bytes.  Note that these are the
890	   sizes of the raw keys, not including ASN.1 encoding overhead from
891	   OneAsymmetricKey and SubjectPublicKeyInfo wrappers.  Private key
892	   sizes are shown for both the seed format and expanded format.

894	   |=======+=======+=====+========+========+==========+==========|
895	   | Level | (k,l) | eta |  Sig.  | Public | Private  | Private  |
896	   |       |       |     |  (B)   | Key(B) | Seed(B)  | Expand(B)|
897	   |=======+=======+=====+========+========+==========+==========|
898	   |   2   | (4,4) |  2  |  2420  |  1312  |    32    |   2560   |
899	   |   3   | (6,5) |  4  |  3309  |  1952  |    32    |   4032   |
900	   |   5   | (8,7) |  2  |  4627  |  2592  |    32    |   4896   |
901	   |=======+=======+=====+========+========+==========+==========|

903	                        Figure 1: ML-DSA Parameters

905	Appendix C.  Examples

907	   This appendix contains examples of ML-DSA private keys, public keys,
908	   certificates, and inconsistent seed and expanded private keys.

910	C.1.  Example Private Keys

912	   The following examples show ML-DSA private keys in different formats,
913	   all derived from the same seed 000102...1e1f.  For each security
914	   level, we show the seed-only format (using a context-specific [0]
915	   primitive tag with an implicit encoding of OCTET STRING), the
916	   expanded format, and both formats together.

918	   NOTE: All examples use the same seed value, showing how the same seed
919	   produces different expanded private keys for each security level.

921	C.1.1.  ML-DSA-44 Private Key Examples

923	   Each of the examples includes the textual encoding [RFC7468] followed
924	   by the so-called "pretty print"; the private keys are the same.

926	C.1.1.1.  Seed Format

928	   -----BEGIN PRIVATE KEY-----
929	   MDQCAQAwCwYJYIZIAWUDBAMRBCKAIAABAgMEBQYHCAkKCwwNDg8QERITFBUWFxgZ
930	   GhscHR4f
931	   -----END PRIVATE KEY-----
932	   SEQUENCE {
933	     INTEGER { 0 }
934	     SEQUENCE {
935	       OBJECT_IDENTIFIER { 2.16.840.1.101.3.4.3.17 }
936	     }
937	     OCTET_STRING {
938	       [0 PRIMITIVE] { `000102030405060708090a0b0c0d0e0f10111213141
939	   5161718191a1b1c1d1e1f` }
940	     }
941	   }

1246	C.1.2.  ML-DSA-65 Private Key Examples

1248	   Each of the examples includes the textual encoding [RFC7468] followed
1249	   by the so-called "pretty print"; the private keys are the same.

3805	C.4.  Example Inconsistent Seed and Expanded Private Keys

3807	      |  WARNING: These private keys are purposely bad do not use them
3808	      |  in production systems.

3810	   The following examples demonstrate inconsistent seed and expanded
3811	   private keys.

3813	   Three ML-DSA-44-PrivateKey examples of inconsistent seed and expanded
3814	   private keys follow:

3816	   1.  The first ML-DSA-PrivateKey example includes the both CHOICE ,
3817	       i.e., both seed and expandedKey are included.  The seed and
3818	       expanded values can be checked for inconsistencies.

3820	   2.  The second ML-DSA-PrivateKey example includes only expandedKey.
3821	       The public key fails to match the tr hash value in the private
3822	       key.

3824	   3.  The third ML-DSA-PrivateKey example also includes only
3825	       expandedKey.  The private s_1 and s_2 vectors imply a t vector
3826	       whose private low bits do not match the t_0 vector portion of the
3827	       private key (its high bits t_1 are the primary content of the
3828	       public key).

3830	   The second and third examples would not be detected by
3831	   implementations that do not regenerate the public key from the
3832	   private key, or neglect to then check consistency of tr or t_0.

4012	Appendix D.  Pre-hashing (ExternalMu-ML-DSA)

4014	   Some applications require pre-hashing that ease operational
4015	   requirements around large or inconsistently-sized payloads.  When
4016	   signing with pre-hashing, the signature generation process can be
4017	   separated into a pre-hash step requiring only the message and other
4018	   public information, and a core signature step which uses the public
4019	   key.

4021	   In the context of ML-DSA, pre-hashing can be performed with the
4022	   HashML-DSA algorithm defined in Section 5.4 of [FIPS204].  ML-DSA
4023	   itself supports a External Mu pre-hashing mode which externalizes the
4024	   message pre-hashing originally performed inside the signing
4025	   operation.  This mode is also laid out in [FIPS204-ExternalMuFAQ].
4026	   This document specifies only the use of ML-DSA's External Mu mode,
4027	   and not HashML-DSA, in PKIX for reasons laid out in Section 10.1.

4029	   Implementations of ML-DSA using the External Mu pre-hashing mode
4030	   requires the following algorithms, which are modified versions of the
4031	   algorithms presented in [FIPS204].  The nomenclature used here has
4032	   been modified from the NIST FAQ [FIPS204-ExternalMuFAQ] for clarity.

4034	   Pre-hash operation:

4036	ComputeMu(pk, M, ctx):

4038	  # Referred to as 'ExternalMu-ML-DSA.Prehash(pk, M, ctx)'
4039	  # in the FIPS 204 FAQ.
4040	  # M is the message, a bit-string
4041	  # mu and ctx are byte-strings.
4042	  # ctx is the context string, which defaults to the empy string.

4044	  mu = H(BytesToBits(H(pk, 64) || IntegerToBytes(0, 1) ||
4045	                IntegerToBytes(|ctx|, 1) || ctx) || M, 64)
4046	  # The functions `BytesToBits` and `IntegerToBytes` are defined in FIPS 204.
4047	  return mu

4049	                Figure 2: ComputeMu prehash operation

4051	   Sign operations:

4053	SignMu(sk, mu):

4055	  # Referred to as 'ExternalMu-ML-DSA.Sign(sk, mu)'
4056	  # in the FIPS 204 FAQ.

4058	  if |mu| != 64 then
4059	    return error  # return an error indication if the input mu is not
4060	                  # 64 bytes.
4061	  end if

4063	  rnd = rand(32)  # for the optional deterministic variant,
4064	                  # set rnd to all zeroes
4065	  if rnd = NULL then
4066	    return error  # return an error indication if random bit
4067	                  # generation failed
4068	  end if

4070	  sigma = SignMu_internal(sk, mu, rnd, isExternalMu=true)
4071	  return sigma

4073	ML-DSA.SignMu_internal(sk, M', rnd, isExternalMu=false):
4074	    # mu can be passed as an argument instead of M'
4075	    # defaulting is ExternalMu to false means that
4076	    # this modified version of Sign_internal can be used
4077	    # in place of the original without interfering with
4078	    # functioning of pure ML-DSA mode.
4079	    # ... identical to FIPS 204 Algorithm 7, but with Line 6 replaced with
4080	  6: if (isExternalMu):
4081	       mu = M'
4082	     else:
4083	       mu = H(BytesToBits(tr) || M', 64)

4085	               Figure 3: The operations for signing mu

4087	   There is no need to specify an External Mu Verify() routine because
4088	   this is identical to the original ML-DSA.Verify().  This makes
4089	   External Mu mode simply an internal optimization of the signer, and
4090	   allows an ML-DSA key to sometimes be used with the "one-shot" Sign()
4091	   API and sometimes the External Mu API without any interoperability
4092	   concens.

4094	   The External Mu mode requires the ComputeMu routine to have access to
4095	   the hash of the signer's public key which may not be available in
4096	   some architectures, or require fetching it.  That may allow for
4097	   mismatches between tr and sk.  At worst, this will produce a
4098	   signature which will fail to verify under the intended public key
4099	   since a compliant Verify() routine will independently compute tr from
4100	   the public key.  That is not believed to be a security concern since
4101	   mu is never used as-is within ML-DSA.Sign_internal() (Algorithm 7 in
4102	   [FIPS204]).  Rather, it is hashed with values unknown to an attacker
4103	   on lines 7 and 15.  Thus, a signing oracle exposing SignMu() does not
4104	   leak any bits of the secret key.  The External Mu mode also requires
4105	   SHAKE256 to be available to the ComputeMu routine.
